Feel free to comment.<br /><br />What's for dinner?<br /><br />The Omnivore's Dilemma, by Michael Pollan (Knight Professor of Journalism at the Graduate School of Journalism at UC-Berkeley and director of the Knight Program in Science and Environmental Journalism ) serves up one of the most thoughtful and unforgettable journeys into what he refers to as three principle food chains: the industrial, the organic, and the hunter-gatherer.  His personal journey through four actual meals deriving from these three food chains enlightens his readers by taking them into the worlds (industrial and organic) of agriculture and food production.  Pollan explores the ever present question of \\"what's for dinner?\\" by delving into the deeper ethical issues that every omnivore ought to consider.<br /><br /> Extremely engaging and pleasant to read, The Omnivore's Dilemma's 411 pages go by surprisingly fast.  Michael Pollan is a winsome and thoughtful writer who has the ability to present an extremely controversial and specialized topic in such a way that the reader is not forced to reading this journey dictionary in hand.  It is evident that Pollan cares deeply about his topic going to extremes to gather information and spending much of his life researching and developing thoughts before they are published.  Pollan has a deep and diverse bibliography that only bolsters his thoughtful presentation of this issue and makes his credibility very difficult to question.<br /><br />The Menu<br /><br />Pollan dives deep into the source of the issues of what he calls \\"America's national eating disorder\\" by tracking the three food chains (see above) all the way to the source of where food begins.  Pollan's journey starts by exploring the world of corn and corn farming by working, temporarily, on two very different farms.  His voyage continues with the purchase of a steer for slaughter, slaughtering or \\"harvesting\\" chickens, hunting wild boar, and foraging for morels in Northern California.<br /><br /> Michael Pollan's chief concern is with the industrial food chain.  This industrial food chain has enabled us to eat our meals without the inconvenience of knowing where our food comes from or what was involved in getting it to our plates.  In Pollan's opinion (which is difficult to refute) this industrial food making process has divorced humans from our natural relationship to our food.  Pollan believes that when we don't consider where our food comes from and how it got to us we are endangering our health, the environment and the order of the natural world.  By venturing into the industrial food chain Pollan reveals to his readers the truth behind corn, one of our most basic crops.  He also reveals the dangers and abuses of CAFOs (Confined Animal Feeding Operations; aka feedlots) along with many other concerns namely: environmental, biological, and economical which are direct results of the arrival of the industrial food chain.<br /><br /> As Pollan shares with us in the introduction his intended readers are those who share his concern for eating properly instead of \\"eating in ignorance.\\"  He does not exclude anyone else who might read this book but he does caution those who don't want change in their eating endeavors because after reading this book their appetites might be ruined.<br /><br /> The introduction of The Omnivore's Dilemma gives us a road map of Pollan's goals with the book.  His construct is to build the book around 4 meals that he actually eats.  His first meal is from the industrial food chain; a dinner with his family from McDonalds which is eaten in the car.  The second meal is from the Industrial Organic (he refers to as an oxymoron) food chain which he eats alone; an organic T.V. dinner.  His third meal is more true to the word organic and is eaten on Joel Salatin's (the owner of Polyface Farm in Virginia where Michael worked one summer) farm made entirely from produce and livestock grown on Joel's farm.  The fourth meal is what he calls \\"the perfect meal\\" which is the hunter-gatherer meal made almost entirely from ingredients that Michael gathered or killed on his own.<br /><br />The Dilemma<br /><br /> The Omnivore's Dilemma was first recommended to me last summer.  I was extremely busy at the time but the person who recommended this book to me is someone that I respect greatly and so I wanted to take his advice seriously.  So I did the next best thing and recommended it to my father-in-law who read it quite quickly and agreed that this book is a must read.  A must read?  That seemed awfully compelling.  I think about the books that are a must read and The Omnivore's Dilemma probably would not have been on the forefront.  Maybe a book on abortion, or same-sex marriage or eating disorders would be up there but a book on food, really, is this necessary?<br /><br /> In his book Pollan makes the statement that \\"Agriculture has done more to reshape the natural world than anything else we humans do...\\"  Really?  Agriculture?  What about wars, religion, works of art or works of literature?  This struck me as quite bold but one would have a difficult time arguing with Michael Pollan after thoughtfully considering the challenges brought forth in his book.<br /><br />The dilemma of The Omnivore's Dilemma is that no matter who we are we cannot ignore the fact that we have to eat.  Our eating does not take place in a void but holds consequences not only for our own bodies but also the world around us.  Considering where food comes from, whether we are eating at McDonalds, Longhorn or shopping at our local grocery store is just the beginning of contemplating these consequences.  We see commercials describing \\"corn fed beef\\" as a term to portray the best kind of meat but in fact cows are ruminants and were never supposed to eat corn.  Thoughtful reflection of what it means to be human should cause us to consider these issues and many other ethical concerns that encompass the question, \\"what's for dinner?\\"<br /><br />The Spiritual Dilemma<br /><br /> Taking inventory of all of the ethical issues there are for Christians in Pollan's book, I was challenged to do some soul searching.  Although not a Christian himself, (his self-renunciation of faith and his continued attribution to evolution as responsible for our development are made clear in his book) Pollan seems to be a delightful person who is deeply concerned for our environmental wellbeing.  This seems to be eons beyond where our current evangelical community is on these issues.  I began to think about what is offered for Christians on the issues of food, agriculture and environment so I perused our local Christian bookstore.<br /><br /> After doing a computer search and looking up words like food, agriculture, and environment, I came up with results like The Total Temple Makeover (apparently a play off of the idea that our bodies are temples with advice as a how to \\"makeover\\" this temple), The Hallelujah Diet, and Stress Less.  A search on agriculture produced nothing and looking for environmental issues brought up only the newest edition of Praise Baby.  It is not my goal to criticize these particular books, having never read them (or listened to them, Praise Baby is a CD).  But if there is nothing on this issue in what we might refer to as the contemporary Christian library this tells me that the Evangelical world is not even thinking about this issue.  This exposes the reality of the stereotypes one might see in the political and scientific world regarding Evangelical Christians.<br /><br /> As Christian Believers we must think about the issues that Pollan brings to the table in regards to ethical eating.  Exploring some of the larger issues that Pollan investigates, I found myself reading mouth wide open.  For example:  My wife and I and most everyone in the area where we live get our food from our local chain grocer.  In any of the packagedfoods that we purchase one or more of the staple ingredients, in some form or another, is corn.  Now this might not seem so bad considering the fact that corn is thought of as a vegetable.  However what we are not aware of is the fact that most of the corn grown in the United States is not a profitable endeavor for the American Farmer.  In fact the face of post-WWII American agriculture has corn smeared all over it, leaving the American farmer without a towel.<br /><br /> When the industrial revolution took hold in the world of agriculture there was no going back.  The result was the creation of the monoculture farm instead of the storybook polyculture farm that we read about in stories like Charlotte's Web or Animal Farm.  The push to for America to grow and flourish drove the USDA to offer incentives for farmers to grow corn and only corn.  They even offered subsidies and payback loans for farmers who grew corn, paying the most to the highest yielding farms.  And so the corn race began in an attempt to satisfy the hunger for beef fueled by corporations realizing the appetites of the American consumer was insatiable.<br /><br /> Corn is used for feed on CAFOs or Confined Animal Feeding Operations.  CAFOs are where thousands of beef cattle are kept in close confinement to cut down on labor costs and promote easy and rapid weight gain in order to increase the slaughter rate (which is about 400 an hour).  But what separates these CAFOs from our wrapped lunch for the family of four patiently waiting in the drive through is nothing less than thousands of miles and veiled, blood stained walls of ignorance.<br /><br /> Pollan offers many other ethical situations to be considered.  The fact that cattle are ruminants and were never created to eat corn is one of them.  The reason that corn was introduced to feed was to increase the animal's fat content which makes for nice \\"marbling\\" of the meat which results in tastier steaks.  Consequently we are convinced that \\"corn fed\\" beef is the best meat there is.  This completely covers up the fact that eating corn produces many medical problems for the steer including acidosis which in a short time can cause the cattle's stomach's to expand with gases killing them with suffocation.<br /><br /> The ethical implications of ignoring the industrial food chain are too numerous to mention.  Of the many ethical issues that one could consider in The Omnivore's Dilemma there were three that stood out to me most prevalent as a Christian Believer.<br /><br />1.  The insatiable and self-centeredness of the American appetite.  I was overwhelmed to read about how American's on average eat one fifth of its meals in the car and one third of all children eat at fast-food outlets everyday.  Super-sized (better to call them over-sized meals) meal portions and pre-packaged foods are creating more waste then ever before.  There is an obesity epidemic like none other and food corporations are the largest corporation in the world.<br /><br /> 2.  The treatment of animals for harvesting.  When Pollan describes the way in which cattle are fed, kept, and then slaughtered was disturbing to me and I began to fully understand why he warns the reader that they would be challenged to change their eating habits after reading this book.  The fact that 400 steer are slaughtered every hour in these industrial slaughter houses is enough cause for concern.  McDonald's has a 5% margin of error for failed kills on the slaughter assembly (better called disassembly line).  What this means is that it is acceptable to the McDonald's corporation if no more than 5% of cattle routed through the assembly line go on to the skinning phase before being properly terminated.  In other words some actually end up skinned alive.  This excludes many other abuses done to cattle, poultry, and pork on these CAFOs.<br /><br />3.  The Better Way.  Pollan describes a better way to live.  As Christians we must consider as our highest calling not only the verbal delivery of the gospel, (which can be done to a fault) but also our handling of what has been given to us.  We are called to live the best we can with what we have been given.  Pollan admits that there is no easy solution to the problems that the industrial food chain has brought to our plate but he does explore a much better way.<br /><br />A Better Way<br /><br /> Genesis 1:28 & 29 tells us that God has given us his creation to use for our enjoyment and the furthering of our humanity.  The principles of stewardship (using what has been given to you in accordance with the guidelines of the giver, in this case God) are extremely important when thinking about the ethical issues presented in The Omnivore's Dilemma.  Jesus also discusses stewardship in Matthew chapter 25.  This is not in direct relation to the earth and eating habits but the principle is presented.  It should then be a consideration of every believer how he or she treats what God has given to them.  The Omnivore's Dilemma offers a much better solution for what many of us take for granted every day.<br /><br /> Evidence from nature and from scripture both give us reasons to delight in diversity.  Jesus was about bringing all men (from every nation) to himself and the gospel call is for anyone who would believe.  Biblical ideas and philosophy center on the receiving of anyone who would believe.  It is the exact opposite of an exclusive environment where only one kind is desired.  The industrial food chain has created a monoculture of agriculture that has left a deep environmental scar on not only the landscape of America but also the way we live our lives.<br /><br /> Michael Pollan finds a better way to do food.  I agree with Pollan that not every issue is easily resolved in regards to the dilemma of the omnivore but it is our responsibility to seek out the better way.  It is unfortunate that this better way comes at a price.  It means that the large corporations of America would not have the profit margins that they currently enjoy.  It would mean that humans would have to reconnect to the ways in which their food is grown and harvested which will cost time and effort.  The better way could mean that we do not eat meat at every meal or find our meals as easily (lazily) available.  It could mean a journey back to the dinner table and the family dinner may take on a renewed face.<br /><br /> In The Omnivore's Dilemma Pollan visits Polyface Farm in Swoope, Virginia.  Polyface Farm is owned an operated by Joel Salatin.  Salatin is a third generation farmer who adopted from his father and grandfather alternative ways of farming.  Polyface Farm consist in a polyculture of many crops, but of all of the crops that Joel would consider himself a farmer of he would choose grass.  Joel is a grass farmer because what Joel sees when he looks on a field of grass is steak, chicken and pork all working together to create the best scenario for farming: best for the ground, best for the animals, and best for humans who consume its produce.<br /><br /> At Polyface Farm there is an environment for farming which encourages and promotes the interconnectedness of humans to their food.  Pollan claims that this interconnectedness has all but vanished due to the arrival of industrial farming.  Evidence of this relationship is seen in the open air slaughter house where anyone can visit and see how chickens are slaughtered, (which is in direct contrast to the closed and unwelcome slaughter houses in the industrial food market) to the way in which Joel and his staff treat all of the animals.  Pollan finds great relief at Polyface and recognizes the environmental impact of such a place actually adds to and improves the health of the land in which it is situated.  This is in stark contrast to the environment of the industrial farm.<br /><br /> Pollan also ventures into the world of hunter-gatherer where he takes to the forest in an effort to find wild game and wild morels.  He finds most of the ingredients he needs for cooking this final meal from his own land or someone else's that he knows.  Pollan recognizes that this meal as well as the meal eaten at Polyface encompass what is sorely lacking in the industrial market:  the experience of knowing, \\"...what it is we're eating, [w]here it came from, [h]ow it found its way to our table, [a]nd what, in true accounting, it really cost.\\"  It is evident from reading The Omnivore's Dilemma that this is not considered by most and the cost is higher than any of us care to know about or for that matter care to pay.<br /><br />So what is for dinner?<br /><br /> There is no easy solution to the problem of the omnivore.  We are by nature able to eat almost anything on the earth albeit avoiding that which is deadly.  We no longer have the dilemma of our past omnivores where the dilemma consisted in differentiating between what foods could kill us or at least make us sick.  But we do have large appetites and lots of people to feed.  There has to be a better way.  We can at least start by exploring what it is that we are putting on our plates, finding out where our meat comes from, and asking what phrases like \\"corn-fed\\" and \\"cage-free\\" really mean.  As Christians we will be held accountable for how we treated what was given to us.  It is clear that God has given us dominion over the created world but this dominion does not imply abuse or neglect.<br /><br /> The Omnivore's Dilemma brings great insight into an issue that most of us will probably never think about.  But if this issue is not explored deeper and by more of us we are inevitably on a track taking us further and further from the source of our food.  The meal-in-a-pill phenomenon is not too far off unless we carefully consider the questions posed in The Omnivore's Dilemma.<br /><br /> The effort to find what is for dinner might take more money and more time but it should be considered.  We cannot consider the call to be good stewards of what God has given us without considering what it is that we are eating.  It is not about boycotting massive food corporations like Cargill and ADM but it is about caring for our families and being concerned for what they eat.  I fully acknowledge that getting away from the industrial food chain in its entirety is almost impossible but there are things we can do to make some change.  The issue of feeding massive cities like New York and Chicago on anything but the industrial food chain is a problem that has no easy answer.  However if we do not consider the industrial status quo we cannot expect the future to be much better.<br /><br />What Michael Pollan presents in The Omnivore's Dilemma are the essential ethical issues that many of us, if we are honest with ourselves, do not want to explore because it could mean a change in our dietary lifestyles.  As Christians we need to know how to wed the principles of scripture concerning stewardship and creation with our responsibility as caretakers of God's created world.  Doing this will help us understand why the omnivore's dilemma is an important issue.<br /><br /> The Omnivore's Dilemma and books like it are challenging and essential.  We need to see a better way and we need to be challenged to think before we act.  There are many world issues and problems that humanitarians need to consider and where the issue of what is for dinner falls on this spectrum is a difficult question.  But it is clear that this issue needs to be addressed.  If we are Christian Believers this issue speaks not only to our world and our culture but to our hearts.  When we consider the cost of what we do and how we act we are taking our faith seriously.  This includes, albeit readily ignored, the daily question of \\"what's for dinner?\\"<br /><br />Scott Herroncovers up the fact that eating corn produces many medical problems for the steer including acidosis which in a short time can cause the cattle's stomach's to expand with gases killing them with suffocation.    <br /> The ethical implications of ignoring the industrial food chain are too numerous to mention. 0753820250|4.0|By all means, the book is well written, and I do not regret having read it. However,I think the book has flaws. Perhaps the beginning and the ideas made me expect too much. Maybe also the author had intended too much with this book (it is actually very ambitious). There are the elements of gothic horror and mystery with the concepts of the Library of Lost Books and of the stranger destroying all copies of the mysterious book The Shadow of the Wind. There I could feel the influences of Borges and Lovecraft, and of Robert Chambers's The King in Yellow. It is also a coming of age story of the narrator a la Palliser's The Quincunx. There are also the backdrops of the Franco dictatorship and of life in bohemian Paris.<br /><br />This book appears to attempt a lot. In the end, it looks like the author got overwhelmed, just wanted to finish the book, and turned it into a romance.<br /><br />That said, the book is good, but don't get too excited over the promises made in the beginning. This book does not give any worldshattering revelations a la Borges, or a comment on reality a la Lovecraft, or horror a la Chambers, or a poignant comment on maturing in a dictatorship a la Closely Obseved Trains. It is a well crefted romance.

B000NVIXDW|4.0|I am a musician and a Buble fan for sure, and appreciate the resurgence of my favorite kind of music. But this CD for me lacks the polish, the layers and the richness that I look for in truly exceptional recordings. It's good of course, because he's so talented. But I find him indulging himself a little bit too much. I want artists to be true to the composer and the essence of the music, not preoccupied with their own vocal ability, and that's the sense I got. Sure that lick or that phrase sounded nice, but is it the best you could come up with if you tried it a few different ways? Did it communicate that thought/feeling the best it could? B000002P5W|5.0|When I first heard snatches of \\"Are You Experienced\\", Hendrix' first album on Philly's premier, local, underground FM rock station of the time, I couldn't believe that here was a black, American expatriate, living in England, producing what had to be ARCHE-typical California psychedelic hard rock/funk like no other person before or after him! There was a confidence in Hendrix' singing/songwriting and guitar playing that NO ONE has equaled...white, black, hispanic or otherwise, since! Though he played sideman to people like Little Richard and James Brown, he was a better musician than ten of those two put together, though their influence on him was obvious. Here was a man who took three idioms: soul/R&B, rock and psychedelia, and melded them together in a unique consumme that no one has been able to duplicate, though Cream and Arthur Lee's Love came closer than most.<br /><br />\\"Axis\\" is a little more introspective than \\"AYE\\"...it's like Jimi was telling us: \\"Here I am, at least now...whaddya think?\\" You hear his feelings about relationships in songs like \\"Little Miss Lover\\", \\"Little Wing\\", \\"Wait Until Tomorrow\\" and \\"You Got Me Floatin'\\". You hear his contemplations on spirituality and sci-fi in \\"EXP\\", \\"Bold As Love\\" and \\"Spanish Castle Magic\\" and social musings in \\"If 6 Was 9\\".<br /><br />His arrangements were original, forceful, cocky and catchy. There was nobody else like Jimi Hendrix...anywhere! And it's a shame we lost him so soon after he hit it big...in fact, like so many OTHER rockers from the Woodstock era, becoming a legend by the time his second album, this one, had come out!<br /><br />In one year's span of time, we lost Hendrix, Jim Morrison, Mama Cass Elliott and Janis Joplin...! Now Joplin, as far as I'M concerned, was no great loss, though by no means am I glad she's dead, but Hendrix and Morrison were truly stellar talents that made the world of art and music poorer with their passing. There were TONS more talented female rock singers than Joplin out there and she was the first to admit it, (Mama Cass being one of them!) But Hendrix, perhaps the greatest black crossover artist ever, and Morrison...the musical reincarnation of William Blake himself, THEY left a gaping hole where some truly great music had been!<br /><br />Maybe if somebody can perfect cloning before their DNA rots TOO thoroughly....e first to admit it, (Mama Cass being one of them!) But Hendrix, perhaps the greatest black crossover artist ever, and Morrison...the musical reincarnation of William Blake himself, THEY left a gaping hole where some truly great music had been!    Maybe if somebody can perfect cloning before their DNA rots TOO thoroughly....

B000EBD9TY|5.0|The unfiltered primacy of 27-year old Marlon Brando (in only his second film) cuts through the feverish, Baroque-style histrionics that define Tennessee Williams' near-poetic masterwork. Brando's mastery over the characterization of Stanley Kowalski comes from a precocious ability to undercut the testosterone-driven braggadocio with a rough-hewn sensitivity tied to Stanley's need for Stella. Directed on all cylinders by Elia Kazan, the 1951 adaptation of the Broadway hit has a somewhat stage-bound feel since most of the action takes place within the environs of the Kowalskis' downscale apartment building in New Orleans' French Quarter. However, screenwriter Oscar Saul seizes on the sexual themes of Williams' play and brings a refreshingly adult view to them (at least for the early 1950's), as Kazan guides the principal actors to powerhouse performances that demand our attention.<br /><br />The relatively small-scale plot focuses on faded Southern belle Blanche DuBois, who comes to visit her younger sister Stella from Mississippi where she held onto a fantasy of gentility and respectability growing up on the family plantation, Belle Reve. Hoping for a safe haven in New Orleans, Blanche is disappointed to see that Stella has married Stanley, an unruly blue-collar worker. Conflict ensues almost immediately between Blanche and Stanley with Stella stuck in the middle. Gradually, Blanche's self-delusions peel away her sanity until a harrowing incident takes her over the edge. Even though Brando dominates every moment he has, Vivien Leigh affectingly counterpoints with one of her most definitive performances as Blanche, the true protagonist of the piece.<br /><br />Intriguingly, 56 years later, the contrast between Leigh's florid, more ornately theatrical approach and Brando's fearlessly instinctual work comes across almost too extreme with the actress looking all the more pretentious by comparison. Only in the shattering climax do they truly seem on equal ground. The real surprise, especially in the now-unexpurgated version, is Kim Hunter, whose comparatively subtle performance as Stella maintains a delicate balance between supportive sister and lust-driven wife. In a marginally smaller role, Karl Malden is ideally cast as mama's boy Mitch, who gets caught up in Blanche's lies only to be victimized by them. Harry Stradling's evocative cinematography and especially Alex North's jazzy musical score add substantively to the atmosphere of the heady melodrama.<br /><br />The two-disc 2006 DVD set is a treasure trove of extras. Even though it lacks a direct connection with the scenes, the commentary track provides historical context with tracks recorded separately with 94-year old Malden and film scholars Rudy Behlmer and Jeff Young. The first disc also includes a number of trailers for Kazan's classic films, including three just for \\"Streetcar\\". Disc two has an informative 75-minute 1994 documentary on the director, \\"Elia Kazan: A Director's Journey\\" and five featurettes focused on various aspects of the movie - its birth as a Broadway play, its translation to film, the struggles with censorship and the Hayes Office, North's music, and of course, Brando. There are rare outtakes included, though the best surprise is a four-minute screen test Brando did for [[ASIN:B0007US7EO Rebel Without a Cause]].ially in the now-unexpurgated version, is Kim Hunter, whose comparatively subtle performance as Stella maintains a delicate balance between supportive sister and lust-driven wife. 0307263118|5.0|Can a book about death, grieving and isolation from life be sensuous? That, most certainly, was the feeling that this exquisitely written book evoked in me.<br /><br />Imagine strolling through a world famous art gallery and gazing at the paintings, many of which you already know so well from books or lectures. No doubt many of the paintings would have somber, dark themes, yet somehow the beauty of the art overwhelms, bringing forth awe, enthusiasm...even joy. That is the feeling that reading John Banville's Man Booker award winning novel, \\"The Sea,\\" was like for this reader.<br /><br />Max Morden, the narrator, is not an easy character to understand or like. It is precisely his strangeness that keeps us reading. We live in his mind for the entire novel, yet somehow the reader can't quite come to terms with what makes Max the miserable, grieving, self-loathing, isolated man he appears to be.<br /><br />By profession, Max is an art historian, apparently involved in researching and writing a scholarly book on Pierre Bonnard. But he thinks of himself as \\"a man of scant talent and scanter ambition.\\" He is disappointed in life, disappointed in his daughter, and disappointed in himself.<br /><br />After the death of his wife, Anna, Max returns to the only other place in his life where he felt a whole person: The Cedars, a seaside villa run as a boarding house. It was here, as a young boy, that he first experienced love and death. It is the intertwining stories of that time, the recent past, and the present that make up the story line of the novel.<br /><br />Max is a man who dreams that he owns a typewriter without the letter \\"i.\\" He prefers dispassionately to observe life. Banville gives us few glimpses of him actually participating in life. For Max, life passes before him like images on a tableau and people seem to exist for him alone and not in their own right. How odd, therefore, that he views himself as being made real only through people.<br /><br />Max marries his wife because she gave him the chance to fulfill his fantasy of himself. He remembers her with great tenderness. Yet the only time in the novel where Max allows himself to rage against his loss is when you find him thinking: \\"how could you go and leave me like this, floundering in my own foulness, with no one to save me from myself. How could you.\\"<br /><br />Usually, the reader is drawn positively to a protagonist. But with \\"The Sea,\\" the reader is drawn irresistibly to the beauty of the text. Eliot, frequently came to my mind while savoring this book.<br /><br />I read a library copy, but when I finished I bought my own copy. 0743537009|5.0|I'm not really a big Stephen King fan and had never been exposed to his work until I listened to this story. I'm not sure why I decided to check out this book. I think it was because I'd seen bits of the movie and wondered what kind of source material could've produced Jack Nicolson's performance. While it sort of scared me, I found it funny and dumb at the same time. The movie was obviously a showcase for Nicolson. I'd heard people say that the book was so much better and boy are they right. The novel gives the necessary back story and character depth that the movie lacks, legitimizing Jack Torrance's descent into madness while commenting on alcoholism, broken marriages, the silent competition between parents for their child's affections, and a host of other subjects. While the story is still somewhat improbable, it is not funny at all. King makes you believe this really could happen while you're reading or listening to the story, and the story will leave quite an impression on you once you've finished it.  <br /> <br />If you've only seen the movie and rush into the book looking for some kind of immediate payoff, know that there is none; however the pacing of this story is excellent. King starts it off slowly, setting everything up, then just when you think something big is going to happen, nothing does. It almost begins to feel as if you're right there in the Overlook with Wendy, Jack, and Danny, feeling a growing sense of isolation, discontent and anger. You get strung along with just enough foreshadowing to keep things interesting and before you know it the story comes to life with a sudden intensity that doesn't let up. When you've finished it you'll appreciate the plotting of the book all the more. <br /> <br />Campbell Scott does a spectacular job with his narration and really adds something to the story without getting in the way. He isn't over dramatic, giving each character a unique voice, but he lets King's words scare you, his voice just compliments them. He does an especially good job of switching between the insane and rational Jack Torrance, so well that you never can tell when he's going to snap again. Definitely one of the best narrations of an audio book I've ever heard. You'll want to listen to this more than once. Buy it.

B00005JPAO|5.0|THE QUEEN is not what I would have suspected to be a great movie, due to its expected docu-stoggy approach and subject matter, but it is -- a great movie. And Helen Mirren's Queen Elizabeth II will forever in my mind be the real queen, she did such a majestic job. The story takes place in the week after Prncess Diana's tragicly premature death in a 1997 Paris automobile crash and leads up to her public funeral. Interspersed with actual news footage of Diana and the grieving crowds, the film goes behind closed doors to look at how the Royal Family and the newly-elected Prime Minister, Tony Blair, reacted to these events. Educational as well as entertaining, THE QUEEN is an absorbing study of how families cope in terrible crisis, in this case, the royal and top political families. THE QUEEN also shows how protocol is handled with the Queen and daily life of her household. Fascinating on many levels, THE QUEEN is one of the best films of the last ten years.

B000MCH5P4|3.0|Babel is a decent film but after viewing it I was surprised it received the critical acclaim it did. I am guessing the star-power presence of Cate Blanchett and Brad Pitt had a lot to do with that. Babel is an often flat film without gripping moments or strong subplots. It tells the story of the interconnectedness (a la Short Cuts, Crash, Pulp Fiction and many others with similarly time-skipping themes) of the lives of four groups of people in far flung locales: rural Morocco, northern Mexico, and the kinetic megapolis of Tokyo. Blanchett's performance as an American tourist critically wounded while on vacation far outshone the supposedly understated but really non-existent acting job turned in by co-star Brad Pitt---here looking both more mature and more stressed-out than I'd ever seen. The most interesting segments of the movie were those that concerned the gentle-hearted Mexican woman who traveled across an international boundary with the young American children she watched for a living, and also that with the deaf-mute Japanese teen who was fiercely seeking identity and recognition, all the while dealing with the aftermath of her mother's suicide. I found Babel almost a letdown considering all I'd expected from it, but I'm glad I did finally get to see it, even if on DVD instead of in theaters.

Once all students in his community turn twelve they are assigned a job by the elders.  Jonah is selected to be the Receiver of Memories, the most honorable of all jobs.  Jonas quickly learns that the life he once considered normal was in fact choice less and everything and everyone was the same.  The Receiver of Memories job is to hold all the memories of many centuries.  Eventually Jonas discovers what feelings like love, pain, joy and anger really are while the rest of the community are spared of this and live in ignorance.  Jonas also discovers colors, seasons, and having the ability to make choices.  But when Jonah discovers that when someone is released it is not a reason to celebrate because instead of being sent \\"Elsewhere,\\" the person is killed. Lowry writes in a manner that the reader is convinced that the other members of the community are really clueless to what true feelings like pain and love are, and that they truly live in a choice less, carefully planned world. People don't marry for love, rather the elders match them, and instead of giving birth to their own children there are birthing mothers, and couples must apply for children.  The characters have rigid and proper lives with a strict set of rules everyone must abide by.  Additionally, children are grouped specifically by age and receive certain rules and responsibilities as each year passes.  The community members will never know hunger, or war, or inclement weather because the Elders and Receiver have planned and controlled all of that.  Last, members of the community do not know what choice, colors, or death is.  Lowry writes the story so well that the story and the characters lives sound believable, and the story is logical and consistent throughout. The story ends openly for the readers to decide if Jonas and Gabe will survive.  Between the open ending and the universal truth that this story brings to life give readers many issues to discuss.  For example one universal truth is that every society is plagued with troubles and dangers that the government can't always control because of our rights and freedom.  If the novel was used in a classroom it might provoke students to examine if the rules in our own society should be changed and choice restricted to make the world safer?  Another question an instructor might pose why or why not does the reader believe if Jonas will survive?  <br /> <br />Another book that connects to The Giver, but contain different characters is Gathering Blue which is about a world similar to Jonah's a world of the future but this time the main character is a handicapped orphan named Kira who is spared because of her gifts as a weaver, making her useful to the guardians. <br />

B000006OAM|5.0|Ophelia is Natalie Merchant's sophomore solo CD. This recording differs from TigerLily in that the record label had greater control of the content and players than the singer would have liked -- leading to her break with Elektra. It also marks the debut of Graham Maby on Electric Guitar; he later goes on to record Motherland and The House Carpenter's Daughter with Merchant.<br /><br />Pete Yanowitz plays drums on TigerLily and Ophelia but was replaced by Matt Chamberlain on Motherland; Allison Miller subsumed Chamberlain's role on The House Carpenter's Daughter. Similar shifts with Natalie's guitarists -- Graham Maby strumming alongside Craig Ross and Todd Vos -- occur on this album. Guest musicians like Lokua Kanza, Daniel Lanois, and Don & Karen Peris accompany Merchant just this once.<br /><br />Her lush songs on Ophelia received wide radio airplay, including Break Your Heart, Kind & Generous, Life Is Sweet, and a truncated version of the eponymous title track. Her shuffling of musicians after Ophelia's release also marked a turn away from commercialism towards the purely artistic for Merchant -- a move welcomed by fans, but probably not her business managers.<br /><br />Essentially, Merchant's best tracks on Ophelia are the ones where she does more than sing. She prods her wurlitzer on the melodic Frozen Charlotte (Track Four) and introduces discord to the chorus. Michelle Kinney's cello and the singer's piano accompaniment on My Skin (Track Five) also underscore the beauty of Merchant's lyrics. I also enjoyed Jay Brunga's acoustic bass on My Skin.<br /><br />Lanois's aching, bleeding, crying, wounded electric guitar on Thick as Thieves is transformative, appearing in the bridge and twisting the track's conclusion into an entirely different song. Yanowitz offers up delicate drums that back Merchant's smooth voice brilliantly here; her keyboard skills are confident and introspective, in perfect counterpoint to the guitars. Michelle Kinney's cello is difficult to pick out but beautiful to absorb.<br /><br />I thought Effigy (Track Nine) was interesting; I enjoyed Merchant's styling on the hammond and wurlitzer, but found the Tibetan chanting to be overwrought. I likewise thought The Living (Track Ten) was great; I'd love to hear Kid Rock tackle a cover of this song. When They Ring the Golden Bells, a French funeral song from 1887, was a strangely fitting closing number.<br /><br />All of Ophelia's eleven songs were thoughtfully recorded at Talking Dwarf Studios in Little Valley, New York. The orchestral reprise of the title track performed by Fretwork is a bonus track recorded at Air Studios in London, United Kingdom. Overall, this is just a gorgeous album. You can tell Merchant's band invested a sufficient amount of effort, emotion, energy, and feeling into these songs.cult to pick out but beautiful to absorb. <br /> <br />I thought Effigy (Track Nine) was interesting; I enjoyed Merchant's styling on the hammond and wurlitzer, but found the Tibetan chanting to be overwrought. B000002HHB|5.0|TigerLily (1995) used to be my favorite Natalie Merchant CD -- that is until she released The House Carpenter's Daughter. These records are polar opposites; there's a mellow West Coast pop sensibility underpinning TigerLily, but unmistakable countrified folk frames out The House Carpenter's Daughter. Merchant should try pop music again.<br /><br />In my opinion, San Andreas Fault (Track One) is TigerLily's strongest cut. Miss Merchant's plaintive wail kicks off this languid track -- suggesting steam train whistles of bygone years -- and her glossy voice is backed by percussion, a vibraphone, and dueling guitars (John Holbrook & Matt Henderson.)<br /><br />Gorgeous accompaniment from Barrie Maguire, Jennifer Turner, and Peter Yanowitz infuses Wonder (Track Two) with emotion. If any track here resembles Merchant's work on In My Tribe, I suppose Wonder fills the bill. Natalie warbles about faith, love, and patience, instilling belief in these desired timeless qualities.<br /><br />Emotional songs like River (Track Four) will get to you. This song is about River Phoenix, the young actor who died from a drug overdose on October 31st, 1993. I briefly knew Joaquin Phoenix and River Phoenix in 1980, so I thank Miss Merchant for recording this tribute.<br /><br />River is followed by Carnival (Track Five), which is the single that achieved dominant radio airplay. I loved Adrian Lopez Guevarra's percussion here; I likewise admired Katell Keineg's backing vocals. But I'm still ambivalent about that last snippet of ambient street noise that Natalie's voice recedes into.<br /><br />Lyrically speaking, Cowboy Romance (Track Eight) does the best job of telling a connection story. Maguire, Turner, and Yanowitz enhance Miss Merchant's sensitively lilting and wavering voice, and Jay Unger's maudlin violin is a deftly added touch; this song is primarily about deception, promises made, and promises abandoned.<br /><br />I adore Jealousy (Track Nine), which (in addition to Maguire, Turner, and Yanowitz) includes John Holbrook's noodling on the Electric Guitar and Organ, and Eric Schenkman's adroit strumming. This is Merchant's strongest vocal on TigerLily; I could listen to it repeatedly, savoring the last six words for hours.<br /><br />Lovely numbers like Where I Go (Track Ten) never achieved heavy radio airplay, but it's certainly the most endearing of Merchant's `water as metaphor' efforts. Again, Adrian Lopez Guevarra adds distinct percussion that is perfectly matched to Merchant's gift for verbal phrasing, and Jennifer Turner's plucky Spanish-tinged guitar.<br /><br />You'll also be blown away by Natalie Merchant's sanguine confessional Seven Years (Track Eleven), which calmly mimics Sinead O'Connor's piercing Nothing Compares 2 U. In this song the chanteuse speaks of devotion, and of being unable to see past a lover, while Michelle Kinney's low-pitched cello labors away dirge like.<br /><br />Personal Aside: \\"Who ever thought the quiet girl in sixth grade would become a stunning singer known around the globe? B000NVIXFK|4.0|Almost all reviews of Snakes & Arrows start out by noting that it is the first new Rush album in five years. Perhaps this is to convey a sense of high expectations to the reader and it should be explained further. Rush's previous album, entitled \\"Vapor Trails\\" heralded the return of a band which had been plagued by tragedy for several years. While the album was disappointing, their re-emergence was nothing less than triumphant. At the time, Rush was given a pass by many people for turning in poor work. \\"Vapor Trails\\" continued to lack the passion that had in fact been sorely missing from all of Rush's efforts since \\"Roll The Bones\\".<br /><br />If \\"Vapor Trails\\" was sub-par, then the supporting Tour was anything but. As documented on \\"Rush In Rio\\" and later on \\"R30\\", Rush seemed to relearn that their reputation and indeed their missing passion was rooted in their abilities as performers. As one watched either of these live DVD's, it was almost impossible to believe that these were the same individuals who had recently delivered \\"Vapor Trails\\". What Rush desperately needed in its rehabilitation from tragedy was to be performing. The energy of their loyal fans could not help but give Rush the kick in the pants that it so desperately needed.<br /><br />Rush is a band with a fairly strong work ethic. They don't tour for the sake of touring. Their philosophy seems to dictate that a tour is really only mitigated by a preceding studio release. With the exception of their 30th anniversary tour, this has largely been the case. Rush is not a band to rest on its past glories. With two tours and two live recordings at their back, Rush could stand back and smell the roses for a moment. They had emerged from the darkest period of their history, and weathered a poor studio performance to emerge at the top of their form thanks to the fans who patiently stood by them. In return, they now had to live up to the expectations of their fans.<br /><br />Snakes & Arrows does more than live up to the expectations of Rush's fans. In many ways, it blows these expectations to smithereens and sends them into orbit. The responsibility for this can be laid not only at the feet of Rush and their fans, but also some unexpected sources of inspiration. No less than Pink Floyd's David Gilmour offered counsel to guitarist Alex Lifeson which spun the Snakes & Arrows writing in a different direction than Rush had taken in many years. In a brief backstage meeting (a first for both of these guitar icons), Gilmour expounded the wisdom of composing on the acoustic guitar rather than its electric cousin. Inspired by this idea, Lifeson went on to compose all of the guitar tracks acoustically. This is perhaps the most important component to the sound of Snakes & Arrows. For the first time since the heady days of \\"Hemispheres\\" and \\"A Farewell To Kings\\", the acoustic guitar is omnipresent. With the gradual abandonment of keyboards, Rush has sounded thinner over the years, and the acoustic padding gives it back the sweetness that it missed.<br /><br />Neil Peart's poly-rhythmic sensibilities are in full bloom on this album. His tutelage under Freddie Gruber has given Rush the honor of being called the \\"only hard/progressive rock band that can rock and swing at the same time.\\" It seems that Gruber's influence, which was overly dominant on Test For Echo, has been fully assimilated into Peart's playing style.<br /><br />Geddy Lee is back and so is his angry, growling Jazz Bass. Lee continues to walk down the path he started exploring early in his career, elevating the Bass to the position of twin lead/rhythm instrument. He had started along this path in the days of \\"Fly By Night\\", but returned to it on his 2000 solo album \\"My Favorite Headache\\". Long gone are the Steinbergers and Wal basses with their active EQ's and indistinct tones. The Fender has been his mainstay for more than 10 years now.<br /><br />When taken together, these three musicians have forged a new synergy in the same spirit that originally made them great. There is little fear on this record. The trio jump into experimental territory with abandon, and while it sometimes fails, it usually does well. The lack of experimentation on their more recent releases created an almost sterile atmosphere. Rush has thankfully released themselves from that prison.<br /><br />Rush has never created a recording of such complete integration of lyrics and music as Snakes & Arrows. Amongst some fans, the subject matter of the album has created controversy. Given the inspiration of the interesting times under which we live, this certainly cannot be unexpected. Peart is not sidestepping the heavy issues on this album. He is hitting them head on and this is bound to ruffle some feathers.<br /><br />The album is largely populated by superlative tracks. The true standouts are the song \\"Armor And Sword\\" and the instrumental piece \\"The Main Monkey Business\\". \\"Armor And Sword\\" starts out with a Crimson-esque riff which gradually tapers into seamless harmonic resolution. Listening to this is a true treat, as we finally get to hear someone resolve a Frippish riff (he certainly never would have - but bless Robert Fripp anyway.) All the while, Lifeson's acoustic guitar strums in the background. It produces a truly amazing effect.<br /><br />\\"The Main Monkey Business\\" is perhaps Rush's best instrumental since \\"La Villa Strangiato\\". Fans may label this assertion as blasphemy (in many ears, YYZ reigns supreme), but it is not without reason. Unlike many of their other instrumentals, this does not sound like a jam. This is a complicated piece, but maintains an overwhelming sense of joy. Neil Peart claims it took him 3 days to learn it - this is unheard of for a man who is one of the quickest studies in the business.<br /><br />The rest of the album is truly a pleasure to listen to. If the listener has the opportunity, he or she should listen to it in its entirety in order. The album rewards deep listening, but also is enjoyable on a more casual level.<br /><br />With \\"Snakes & Arrows\\", Rush has proved that they do not exist in a world of diminished expectations. They view expectations as something to surpass.synergy in the same spirit that originally made them great. There is little fear on this record. The trio jump into experimental territory with abandon, and while it sometimes fails, it usually does well. The lack of experimentation on their more recent releases created an almost sterile atmosphere. Rush has thankfully released themselves from that prison. <br /> <br />Rush has never created a recording of such complete integration of lyrics and music as Snakes & Arrows. Amongst some fans, the subject matter of the album has created controversy. Given the inspiration of the interesting times under which we live, this certainly cannot be unexpected. Peart is not sidestepping the heavy issues on this album. He is hitting them head on and this is bound to ruffle some feathers. <br /> <br />The album is largely populated by superlative tracks. The true standouts are the song \\"Armor And Sword\\" and the instrumental piece \\"The Main Monkey Business\\". \\"Armor And Sword\\" starts out with a Crimson-esque riff which gradually tapers into seamless harmonic resolution. Listening to this is a true treat, as we finally get to hear someone resolve a Frippish riff (he certainly never would have - but bless Robert Fripp anyway.) All the while, Lifeson's acoustic guitar strums in the background. It produces a truly amazing effect. <br /> <br />\\"The Main Monkey Business\\" is perhaps Rush's best instrumental since \\"La Villa Strangiato\\". Fans may label this assertion as blasphemy (in many ears, YYZ reigns supreme), but it is not without reason. Unlike many of their other instrumentals, this does not sound like a jam. This is a complicated piece, but maintains an overwhelming sense of joy. Neil Peart claims it took him 3 days to learn it - this is unheard of for a man who is one of the quickest studies in the business. <br /> <br />The rest of the album is truly a pleasure to listen to. If the listener has the opportunity, he or she should listen to it in its entirety in order. The album rewards deep listening, but also is enjoyable on a more casual level. <br /> <br />With \\"Snakes & Arrows\\", Rush has proved that they do not exist in a world of diminished expectations. They view expectations as something to surpass. <br />

B000002JSP|4.0|In the wake of punk and disco, Led Zeppelin in 1979 must have looked very old.  For what would end up being their last album, \\"In Through the Out Door\\", the band responded by embracing synthesizers and new sounds.  This may have had more to do with the dominant voice on the record being bassist John Paul Jones rather than guitarist Jimmy Page, who was absent for large portions of the sessions, but under Jones' guidance, the band produced perhaps their most unique album. <br /> <br />This change in sound is apparent from the outset-- the churning, ambient noise intro of \\"In the Evening\\" signals the change before cascading in a descending, sweeping dramatic riff that recalls the best of \\"Physical Graffiti\\".  Topping off a great fierce vocal by Robert Plant amidst the keyboard swirl is a fantastic, almost blunting solo by Page.  The rest of the record doesn't stick to this path though, exploring everything from a feature for Jones' boogie piano (the superb \\"Southbound Saurez\\"), an electric rock take on country and western (the highly entertaining \\"Hot Dog\\") and a couple absolutely fantastic pop songs (the lilting \\"Fool in the Rain\\", with a fuzz soaked solo from Page, the tender, synth-string driven power ballad \\"All My Love\\").  Along the way, there's even an extended cut (\\"Carouselambra\\"), that while it's long and melodramatic, manages to hold interest and a great blues closer (\\"I'm Gonna Crawl\\"). <br /> <br />\\"In Through the Out Door\\" is probably not for everybody, it's a very different Led Zeppelin, but I've often thought it was among the best work they've ever done.  Highly recommended.

There's enough good material on here to make it worth having, but this is not among the band's finer moments.

B000EMGAOY|4.0|I agree with the reviewer who said that you shouldn't review the album until you've heard the whole thing. So I'm not going to review the music, but this is a review of the album.    This is from the Web site announcing the vinyl release:     \\"The original analog master tapes were driven out to Acous Tech Mastering in Camarillo, CA. where the legendary vinyl mastering team of Kevin Gray and Steve Hoffman cut Stadium Arcadium across 8 sides. The first step was to get the incredible sound on those master tapes transferred on to lacquers and as you will hear that was flawlessly accomplished. Next up was the plating and pressing process where all we had to do was go across the hall to R.T.I. which is known around the world for their high quality vinyl making.\\"    The vinyl came in two different sets. One, on 180-gram vinyl with a big book and stuff inside for ninety-nine bucks, the other on what they called regular vinyl with no booklet, just four discs for thirty-nine bucks. I got the latter for thirty-six bucks while visiting in Louisville and it is one of the finest packaging jobs I've ever seen for any record. It's almost stately, especially for a rock album. If the vinyl in the package I bought is inferior, they fooled me. It weighs a ton and is gorgeous.   People complaining about the mastering on the CD ought to, because the disc is a typical modern compression disaster. The vinyl is completely different.     From Wikipedia: \\"Similar to Californication, Vlado Meller's mastering of Stadium Arcadium features a heavy use of audio compression. However, the vinyl versions of Stadium Arcadium have been mastered by Steve Hoffman and Kevin Gray at AcousTech from the 30 ips analog master tapes,[5] \\"the mixes are all powerful, very dynamic, tuneful, exciting and quite high fidelity sounding.\\"    I've played one record, both sides, so far, and I just wanted to come on and let people know that the production is incredible. I thank my lucky stars I didn't abandon vinyl for CD(I have a nice CD player) or, gasp, iPod. The new generation coming in that doesn't care about fidelity in music is just a wave of tone-deaf dummies, as far as I'm concerned. I'm not an audio snob, but I want my music to sound great and have wanted it to ever since the old high school days when we'd go to the person's house with the best stereo to cue up the Yes and Genesis and all that. It's supposed to sound great. On Vinyl, Stadium Arcadium does. For the best selling album of 2006, why settle for less? Go buy a record player, folks, and find out what you've been missing, or forgot. I would love if someone posted here why they don't care about the quality of the production when listening to music they purport to love . . .

B000B5IPDI|5.0|Tim Burton's return to genuine film making is a welcome endeavor indeed. Here he creates a film that reminds me of what great film making is all about: fantasy, love and reflecting on the human spirit. I scoffed at a review that compared Big Fish to The Wizard of Oz when Big Fish first came out, but upon viewing it the comparison is really not hyperbolic at all and is actually quite justified. There is a unique carelessness and an innocence that resides perfectly and constantly in both films. To me, both films are truly a breath of fresh air and hope.<br /><br />Big Fish is a book written by Daniel Wallace and is the delightful story of Edward Bloom, who has reached the twilight of his life and surrounds himself with his son, daughter-in-law and his wonderful wife Sandra. Eddie has seemingly lived a fantastic life of lies and exaggerations and his son has grown to call his bluff on more than one occasion. In fact, his son returns not just to possibly say good-bye to his father, but to attempt to get him to spill the beans on the truth of who his old man really is. Eddie of course, stands by his stories and brushes off his son's accusations nonchalantly. Most of the film we see Eddie revisit his life as a whole, seen through only his own stories. How he once befriended a 12 foot man; how he arrived in a town that was paradise, once to early and once too late when he turned it back into paradise again; how he joined the circus for three years so he could find out pieces information once a month from Amos the ringmaster about the girl Eddie was sure would be his wife and how Sandra would believe Eddie to be dead in war but he would return. The stories are full of details that would clearly indicate they are false but sometimes they are just better that way. Eddie is a mythological figure and that is just fine with him and as a viewer it's fine with me as well.<br /><br />Eddie is played by Albert Finney who is in turn mirrored by Eddie's youthful version, the outstanding Ewen MacGregor who once again proves his versatility. Jessica Lange plays the older Sandra and she is played as a youngster by the talented Alison Lohman who carries as much energy and beauty as you could expect for a role with so little dialogue and so much importance. She is a real find and makes you fall in love with her right along with Eddie. Helena Bonham Carter brings her talents to the roles of The Witch and Jenny (or all of the other important women in Eddie's life). Steve Buscemi shows up, which is always a pleasant surprise and of course Amos is played by Danny DeVito who is as enjoyable as ever. The flat Keanu Reeves clone Billy Crudup is perhaps the only drawback, but he is a safe casting call as Eddie's son and does what he can in discovering that his father is exactly what he says he is and more.<br /><br />Let me just add that I believe Big Fish is a family film. I don't see why it shouldn't be rated PG rather than PG-13. The language rises above the prime time television level once, there is blood only in a comedic and romantic fight sequence that has a truly admirable message and there is a women's nude rear displayed briefly and non-sexually. This is not grounds for a PG-13 movie. I would bring a seven year old to see this. In fact, my guess is that the movie was directed at this demographic. When content is not exploitative, it is not really inappropriate. I can't see why Rock Diesel films get PG-13ed when the message is nothing short of \\"Kill the bad guys, make a lame joke, drive and crash really cool vehicles and get the dirty chick\\". Anyway, Big Fish may be about a guy who is stretching the truth but the characters' hearts couldn't be more firmly in the right place. The scene when Eddie fills an entire field with Sandra's favorite flower and stands in the middle of the field, outside of her window and calls out to her comes to mind. It brings joy to my heart in a way that only a film like The Wizard of Oz can, and a small child should neverever miss that kind of message. Big Fish is a smart film that really generates a ton of emotion and convincing special effects. I don't doubt for a moment that more work went into the effects than money. This film carried a sense of hope, pride, real love, respect, fantasy and the crucial element that films of these tainted times often forget: natural and unforced optimism.<br /><br />Then there is Tim Burton. He is the filmmaker that can put all of these elements together and for the first time tug at your emotions as well. Two things make this film better than Burton's other work. Firstly, it is real and doesn't dwell on being over-stylized and under-dramatized. Secondly, it is pure, clean and full of moments we can all relate too. Tim Burton has made a film that will alienate his older fans who haven't matured like he has, without \\"selling out\\" (he's done that before) and he has made a film that the whole world can watch, enjoy and discover this unique filmmaker. I'm glad that he saved some of his real film making inspiration for this wonderful little story.of message. B00005JPFF|3.0|\\"Spiderman 3\\" falls victim to a common ailment of most hero movie franchises. If one villain is good, then two would be great, But if two would be great, then three would be fabulous! (Think the horribly overstuffed \\"[[ASIN:0790732912 Batman & Robin]]\\" or \\"Superman III.\\") So while I really enjoyed the third installment in the Spiderman series, I kept getting the feeling that I was watching what would have made two great movies crammed into a single average one.<br /><br />For instance, is Thomas Haden Church's Sandman necessary to this film? Granted he lets the dark Spiderman loose for a bit, but both The New Goblin and Venom get their turns as bad spidey bait. It also sucked points away by convoluting plot points and weakly re-writing the circumstances of Uncle Ben's death. The movie's pace would have significantly improved had the conflicts been more limited to Peter, Harry and Eddie Brock. Since the main focus was on Harry and Peter's attention to Mary Jane and Eddie's anger at losing his girl to Peter, it would have tightened the story if Sandman had been saved for later.<br /><br />The conflict also gives Tobey Maguire a chance to goof around with the role. His moments as Dark Peter Parker are some of the film's funniest (following J.K. Simmons as J. Johah Jameson), and his antics in the jazz-cafe were the ones that got the big reaction in the theater I was watching this. I also found it amusing that when Peter gets bad his bangs suddenly fall down his face and he looks like he should be joining a Fall Out Boy/My Chemical Romance concert.<br /><br />What all this crowding does accomplish is a shortchanging of character development. How Sandman and Venom form an alliance in zero seconds flat is whiplash inducing as is the almost total lack of development in Topher Grace's character. He's brought in so quickly you wonder how he became so angry so fast in his evolution into Venom. Aunt May is barely in the plot at all; Peter's apartment manager and daughter get more significance. Harry/The New Goblin plays a major role throughout, but his character stages are done so abruptly that, once again, you end up wondering what the heck just happened here.<br /><br />This probably won't distract you from all the action, and Sam Raimi again delivers the goods. The flights, fights and special effects are astonishing as ever. Spiderman's suits are as sleek and cool as before, and all the gimmicks will keep your eyes on the screen. In the case of Vapor Trails, it took about four years. Snakes and Arrows probably won't take as long, but still--how many groups have been together for 30+ years, with the same line-up, and offer material that warrants such close and lengthy study? I can't think of any. Mugs in the air for Rush!

B000NVIXFK|4.0|When new music finds its way into our soundscape, we sometimes find it difficult to make it fit.  As we get more engaged in our listening habits over the course of our lives, it sometimes takes a degree of patience to really decide as to whether an album is worth the trouble or not.  Inevitably, the majority of the music that we are initially enthusiastic about seems to have a short life in the player and, conversely, music that we don't understand at first (and subsequently invest ourselves in) tends to keep our attention in the long run.  As a result of our increasingly short attention spans this latter music is highly criticized and often dismissed.  This phenomenon is manifested in the seeming desire to seek fault rather than strength in the music we listen to, without giving ourselves the opportunity to dwell with the music and come to an understanding of its meaning.<br /><br /> This problem is particularly exacerbated when a band's longevity has ensured their music's place in the soundtrack of so many people's lives.  There is an inevitable tendency to rely on our nostalgic recontexualization as an ideal, closing ourselves to the possibilities of the music in front of us.  Many people, including myself, struggle with this issue in regards to Rush.  I was exposed to Rush through \\"Power Windows,\\" which is one of my all-time favorite albums.  I came to love everything before that album, but I struggled with \\"Hold Your Fire,\\" as it did not fit within my concepts of what Rush did or what they had done before.  As I have talked to other Rush fans, they tend to similarly romanticize their initial experiences with Rush.  Their first experiences, and the history that led up to them, define their ideas about the band.  Subsequent releases by the band tend to challenge the listener's perception.<br /><br />With this in mind, I have an undeniable urge to compare \\"Snakes & Arrows\\" with \\"Moving Pictures\\" and \\"Power Windows,\\" but ultimately I have to accept that this  aspect of Rush's repertoire is informed by romanticized recollections of my youth.  Thanks to the rose-colored glasses of nostalgia, things are NEVER as good as they once were.<br /><br /> In some ways, Rush addresses this issue on \\"Snakes & Arrows\\" very directly.  The obvious harmonic references to their \\"Hemispheres\\" era is palpable throughout the album, as is exemplified in \\"Far Cry,\\" \\"Armor and Sword,\\" and \\"Faithless,\\" while the arrangements of the multiple instrumental tracks \\"The Main Monkey Business\\" and particularly \\"Malignant Narcissism\\" recall the \\"Moving Pictures\\" era.  In some ways, these references admittedly teeter on the brink of manipulation.  On my first listening, I felt that Rush was effectively sampling themselves in an effort to establish their own authenticity, and I am still not totally convinced that this is not the case.<br /><br /> However, \\"Snakes & Arrows\\" is hardly a rehash of Rush's early work.  Instead, subsequent listenings reveal it to be a compelling synthesis of a significant body of their repertoire.  It has much of the short-form compositional complexity that \\"Power Windows\\" and \\"Hold Your Fire\\" exhibits, but it also represents the song-based approach exemplified by \\"Counterparts\\" and \\"Test for Echo.\\"  Within the harmonic landscape of their early work, this blend of Rush's compositional strengths and their more recent lyrical approach is aurally compelling.<br /><br />After all, it is not uncommon for musicians and bands with a long history to unify their output into a singular sound.  Peter Gabriel's \\"Up,\\" King Crimson's \\"The Power to Believe,\\" and, in some ways, TooL's \\"10,000 Days\\" are all examples of this tendency.  \\"Snakes & Arrows\\" attends to this goal in ways that are very subtle.  Without taking the time to unravel what the album is trying to say both musically and lyrically in the context of Rush's history, the passive listener may not allow themselves to be open to the album's intent.<br /><br /> As a brief aside, the lyrical content of \\"Snakes and Arrows\\" is undoubtedly shows Peart as his most personal.  Although I do not think that he has written his definitive novel yet, reading his written work to date provides insight into the circumstances that led him to address concepts of \\"faith\\" and \\"belief\\" for this album.  Understanding his text gives him a specific authority that imbues lines like \\"No one gets to Heaven without a fight\\" with a certain weight.  Despite the elitist attitude that many people read into his text, I have a certain amount of sympathy for his hidden insecurity that seems to breed this attitude. Personally, I think that many people misread his defensive insecurity as elitism, but having never met him beyond his writing, this is purely conjecture.<br /><br />The Lowdown:  Rush fans will undoubtedly be divided on \\"Snakes & Arrows,\\" as it refers to everything before it while simultaneously defying the ideas surrounding Rush's repertoire.  The most devoted fan will come to understand it, while many others will dismiss it because the attention that it requires is the same as the kind of attention they might have paid to their older sibling's singular copy of \\"Moving Pictures,\\" which played again and again beyond their control.  This musico-cultural relationship is not available today.aside, the lyrical content of \\"Snakes and Arrows\\" is undoubtedly shows Peart as his most personal. Despite the elitist attitude that many people read into his text, I have a certain amount of sympathy for his hidden insecurity that seems to breed this attitude. Personally, I think that many people misread his defensive insecurity as elitism, but having never met him beyond his writing, this is purely conjecture.    <br /> <br />The Lowdown:  Rush fans will undoubtedly be divided on \\"Snakes & Arrows,\\" as it refers to everything before it while simultaneously defying the ideas surrounding Rush's repertoire.  The most devoted fan will come to understand it, while many others will dismiss it because the attention that it requires is the same as the kind of attention they might have paid to their older sibling's singular copy of \\"Moving Pictures,\\" which played again and again beyond their control.
